The running example we’ll be working with in this section is the day-to-day\nﬂuctuations in weather patterns. Our weather model will be time-dependent (as are Markov models in\ngeneral), meaning we’ll have a separate random variable for the weather on each day. If we deﬁne Wi as the\nrandom variable representing the weather on day i, the Markov model for our weather example would look\nlike this:\nWhat information should we store about the random variables involved in our Markov model? To track'), Document(metadata={'source': 'E:\\FYP\\code\\TALIM\\Data\\AI-Lec 10 Notes.pdf', 'file_path': 'E:\\FYP\\code\\TALIM\\Data\\AI-Lec 10 Notes.pdf', 'page': 0, 'total_pages': 12, 'format': 'PDF 1.4', 'title': 'n8.pdf', 'author': '', 'subject': '', 'keywords': '', 'creator': 'Preview', 'producer': 'macOS Version 11.5.2 (Build 20G95) Quartz PDFContext', 'creationDate': "D:20230917164534Z00'00'", 'modDate': "D:20230917164534Z00'00'", 'trapped': '', 'Instructor_name': 'Dr. Seemab Latif'}, page_content='What information should we store about the random variables involved in our Markov model? To track\nhow our quantity under consideration (in this case, the weather) changes over time, we need to know both\nit’s initial distribution at time t = 0 and some sort of transition model that characterizes the probability\nof moving from one state to another between timesteps. The initial distribution of a Markov model is\nenumerated by the probability table given by Pr(W0) and the transition model of transitioning from state i to\ni+1 is given by Pr(Wi+1|Wi). Note that this transition model implies that the value of Wi+1 is conditionally\ndependent only on the value of Wi. In other words, the weather at time t = i + 1 satisﬁes the Markov\nproperty or memoryless property, and is independent of the weather at all other timesteps besides t = i.\nUsing our Markov model for weather, if we wanted to reconstruct the joint between W0, W1, and W2 using\nthe chain rule, we would want:'), Document(metadata={'source': 'E:\\FYP\\code\\TALIM\\Data\\AI-Lec 10 Notes.pdf', 'file_path': 'E:\\FYP\\code\\TALIM\\Data\\AI-Lec 10 Notes.pdf', 'page': 0, 'total_pages': 12, 'format': 'PDF 1.4', 'title': 'n8.pdf', 'author': '', 'subject': '', 'keywords': '', 'creator': 'Preview', 'producer': 'macOS Version 11.5.2 (Build 20G95) Quartz PDFContext', 'creationDate': "D:20230917164534Z00'00'", 'modDate': "D:20230917164534Z00'00'", 'trapped': '', 'Instructor_name': 'Dr. Seemab Latif'}, page_content='the chain rule, we would want:\nPr(W0,W1,W2) = Pr(W0)Pr(W1|W0)Pr(W2|W1,W0)\nHowever, with our assumption that the Markov property holds true and W0\n|=\nW2|W1, the joint simpliﬁes to:\nPr(W0,W1,W2) = Pr(W0)Pr(W1|W0)Pr(W2|W1)\nAnd we have everything we need to calculate this from the Markov model. More generally, Markov models\nmake the following independence assumption at each timestep: Wi+1\n|=\n{W0,...,Wi−1}|Wi. This allows us to\nreconstruct the joint distribution for the ﬁrst n+1 variables via the chain rule as follows:\nPr(W0,W1,...,Wn) = Pr(W0)Pr(W1|W0)Pr(W2|W1)...Pr(Wn|Wn−1) = Pr(W0)\nn−1\n’\ni=0\nPr(Wi+1|Wi)\nA ﬁnal assumption that’s typically made in Markov models is that the transition model is stationary. In\nother words, for all values of i, Pr(Wi+1|Wi) is identical. This allows us to represent a Markov model with\nonly two tables: one for Pr(W0) and one for Pr(Wi+1|Wi).\nCS 188, Fall 2018, Note 8\n1\nM\nW1\nW2\nW3'), Document(metadata={'source': 'E:\\FYP\\code\\TALIM\\Data\\AI-Lec 10 Notes.pdf', 'file_path': 'E:\\FYP\\code\\TALIM\\Data\\AI-Lec 10 Notes.pdf', 'page': 1, 'total_pages': 12, 'format': 'PDF 1.4', 'title': 'n8.pdf', 'author': '', 'subject': '', 'keywords': '', 'creator': 'Preview', 'producer': 'macOS Version 11.5.2 (Build 20G95) Quartz PDFContext', 'creationDate': "D:20230917164534Z00'00'", 'modDate': "D:20230917164534Z00'00'", 'trapped': '', 'Instructor_name': 'Dr. Seemab Latif'}, page_content='The Mini-Forward Algorithm\nWe now know how to compute the joint distribution across timesteps of a Markov model. However, this\ndoesn’t explicitly help us answer the question of the distribution of the weather on some given day t. Nat-\nurally, we can compute the joint then marginalize (sum out) over all other variables, but this is typically\nextremely inefﬁcient, since if we have j variables each of which can take on d values, the size of the joint\ndistribution is O(d j). Instead, we’ll present a more efﬁcient technique called the mini-forward algorithm.\nHere’s how it works. Given such a table, we could use dynamic programming to compute the best path in\npolynomial time. States in MDPs are represented in the same way as states in traditional search\nproblems.\n• A set of actions A. Actions in MDPs are also represented in the same way as in traditional search\nproblems.\n• A start state.\n• Possibly one or more terminal states.\n• Possibly a discount factor g. We’ll cover discount factors shortly.\n• A transition function T(s,a,s0). This is more than we can say for when we ran value iteration on the\nsame MDP, which was still several iterations from convergence after the two updates we performed.\nSummary\nThe material presented above has much opportunity for confusion. We covered value iteration, policy iter-\nation, policy extraction, and policy evaluation, all of which look similar, using the Bellman equation with\nsubtle variation. Below is a summary of when to use each algorithm:\n• Value iteration: Used for computing the optimal values of states, by iterative updates until conver-\ngence.\n• Policy evaluation: Used for computing the values of states under a speciﬁc policy.\n• Policy extraction: Used for determining a policy given some state value function. Yet, its execution is very\nsimilar to depth-ﬁrst search and it’s time complexity is identical, a dismal O(bm). Recalling that b is the\nbranching factor and m is the approximate tree depth at which terminal nodes can be found, this yields\nfar too great a runtime for many games. For example, chess has a branching factor b ⇡35 and tree depth\nm ⇡100. The downside of this approach is that it may require the\ngeneration of a very large number of samples in order to perform analysis of unlikely scenarios. If we\nwanted to compute P(C|−t), we’d have to throw away 99% of our samples.\nOne way to mitigate this this problem, we can modify our procedure to early reject any sample inconsistent\nwith our evidence. We call this approach rejection sampling.\nThese two approaches work for the same reason, which is that any valid sample occurs with the same\nprobability as speciﬁed in the joint PDF. In other words, the probability of every sample is based on the\nproduct of every CPT, or as I personally call it, the "every CPT participates principle".\nA more exotic approach is likelihood weighting, which ensures that we never generate a bad sample. In\nthis approach, we manually set all variables equal to the evidence in our query. For example, if we wanted\nto compute P(C| −t), we’d simply declare that t is false. The problem here is that this may yield samples\nthat are inconsistent with the correct distribution.
